Preface
This MasterтАЩs Thesis represents a partial fulfillment of the graduation requirements for
a Master of Science, in Civil Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark, DTU.
The project was conducted under the guidance of Associate Professor Philip Skov Halding
at DTU. This project is credited as 30 ECTS points, and the project took place from

Thanks to my supervisor Philip Skov Halding for helping me throughout the project and
always being motivating and positive. Thanks to my brothers for helping me read through
the thesis and encouraging me during this project.
i
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
The arch has been a fundamental architectural form for centuries, known for its ability
to effectively resist loads through compression. The Technical University of Denmark
has developed the Pearl-Chain (PC) technology, which aims to innovate traditional arch
structures using precast concrete and post-tensioning techniques. This technology involves
pre-fabricated super-light concrete decks that are post-tensioned to form a cohesive arch
structure. Building upon the PC technology, the Sandwich Arch Bridge concept has been
explored, integrating a filled spandrel arch bridge with a Pearl-Chain arch, filling layer,
and top plate to create a composite structure, thereby enhancing the overall load-bearing
capacity. This thesis builds on existing research into the concrete sandwich bridge system
by investigating the potential modifications to the top plate and filling layer shapes.
The primary focus is on reducing the environmental impact, specifically Global Warming
Potential (GWP), while retaining structural capacity.
Through extensive calculations
and testing of various parameters, the study found that a curved top deck design offers
advantages in reducing GWP compared to a flat top deck in a sandwich arch bridge.
As an experimental project, this thesis explores the minimal material usage that can be
achieved in sandwich arch bridges, while acknowledging that it does not fully comply with
all existing regulations and standards for curvature and road steepness.

==PAGE:8==
1 Introduction
1
Introduction
1.1
Background
Bridges have been integral to human infrastructure for thousands of years, evolving from
simple structures made of stone and wood to complex designs using advanced materials.
Throughout history, bridge construction has mirrored advancements in materials and tech-
niques, from Roman aqueducts to modern steel and concrete structures. The first known
arch bridge, dating back to 1300 B.C., exemplifies the early engineering ability in utiliz-
ing compression and tension principles [1]. Arch bridges, characterized by their curved
structure, efficiently distribute loads, allowing for longer spans and greater load-bearing
capacity with lower material usage. Today, bridge design not only focuses on structural
integrity and functionality but also considers environmental impact, construction time,
and economic factors.
In this regard, Professor Kristian Hertz from the Technical University of Denmark (DTU)
developed a structural concept known as Superlight Structures (SL-deck). This innova-
tion enables the construction of decks with high stiffness and low weight[2]. With this
new technology, it was possible to have longer spans and a the same time use less materi-
als. In addition to Superlight Structures, Professor Hertz invented the Pearl-Chain (PC)
bridge. This design uses multiple pre-fabricated concrete elements with built-in curved
ducts, which are post-tensioned to form a PC-arch, enhancing the structural integrity and
efficiency of the bridge [3].
In 2015, the first PC-bridge was constructed. This bridge featured three spans: two side
spans of 9 meters and a main span of 13 meters.
Designed as a sandwich bridge, it
included a post-tensioned in-situ cast top plate, a PC-arch, and a filling layer of pervious
concrete between the top plate and the PC-arch. The bridge consisted of a total of 12
arches, which were pre-assembled at the factory and transported to the construction site.
All the arches were lifted into place within half a working day, significantly enhancing the
cost-effectiveness of the construction process [4].
The PC-arch bridge has been investigated further by professor Philip Skov Halding, who
found out that the PC-arch concept could be improved by optimizing the shape and
geometry of the bridge and using post-tensioned SL-decks as a concrete arch [4]. The
PC sandwich arch bridge has been further investigated through various MasterтАЩs thesis
projects under the guidance of Professor Philip Skov Holding. Synne L├╕vold studied the
sandwich effect of the bridge by testing it as both a beam and a 3-hinged arch under
live load conditions [5]. Fredrik Hole Flaa and Sondre Tjetland evaluated the capacity
of the sandwich bridge as a 2-hinge arch. They compared the PC sandwich bridge to
a conventional concrete three-span bridge in terms of total Global Warming Potential
(GWP). Their findings indicated that the sandwich arch bridge has a lower GWP than
the traditional three-span bridge.
For future research, they suggested optimizing the
shape of the top deck to reduce the height of the filling at the ends of the sandwich arch
bridge. This optimization could potentially further decrease the weight of the filling and
the overall GWP [6].
1
==PAGE:9==
1 Introduction
1.2
Scope
The scope of this thesis is to investigate opportunities to optimize the PC sandwich bridge
top deckтАЩs shape to reduce the height of the filling layer at the ends of the sandwich
structure, improving material usage and emissions. This involves:
тАв Evaluating the potential for maintaining a uniform height of the filling layer across
the entire bridge span.
тАв Testing various bridge parameters such as material, dimensions, and geometry to
see how it affect the sandwich arch bridge.
тАв Determine the minimum height of the filling layer and the size of the top deck and
arch elements required to resist bending moments from self-weight and live loads.
тАв Analyse the sandwich arch bridge with different spans.
тАв Comparing the total CO2 emissions of an optimized PC sandwich bridge with a
varied top deck to a PC sandwich bridge with a flat top deck.
1.3
Thesis guide
The outline of the Thesis is divided into different chapters. The chapters are described
in the following points to get an overview of the subjects
тАв Chapter 2: A review of existing and relevant theory within the topics of arch
bridges, PC technology, SL-deck, Sandwich structure and Sandwich arch bridges,
along with some limitations and loads.
тАв Chapter 3: Consist of analytical calculations of optimization of a PC-arch to be
used in the Sandwich arch bridge structure.
тАв Chapter 4: Finding the critical load and load combination from load model 1 and
3. Checking the critical load with 2-hinged and 3-hinges hand calculations with
Robot Structural Analysis.
тАв Chapter 5: Showing how the internal forces react to the critical load case.
тАв Chapter 6: Testing different parameters of the bridge, such as the different height
of different elements and filling, post-tension, and filling material.
тАв Chapter 7: The discussion of results from chapter 5 and the reasons why the
different sizes of the parameters are chosen. Some ideas to make the sandwich arch
bridge with a curved top deck realistic.
2
==PAGE:10==
2 Theory
2
Theory
In this chapter, some general theory about arch structures and shapes regarding an arch
will be presented. A short introduction to different elements that will be used which is
the pearl-chain principle, sandwich structures and SL-decks. Then a brief overview of the
required loads and loads combination that will be used for later calculations. The theory
acts as a foundation for further calculations.
2.1
Arch structures
In architecture and civil engineering, an arch is defined as a vertically curved and axially
compressed structural member used to span openings and support loads from above [7].
Arch structures are advantageous due to their load carrying mechanism, where uniform
loads are primarily carried in compression, or in the case of a true arch by pure com-
pression. In a true arch, dead loads induce mainly axial forces, while non-uniform and
live loads will also induce bending moments [8, 9]. With compression being the primary
force acting on the section, a variety of materials, including timber, masonry, concrete,
metal and composites, can be used in their construction. Concrete, due to its favorable
relation between its production cost and compressive strength, is particularly suitable for
application in arch structures [7, 10].
Figure 2.1 | Load transfer in an arch structure
Due to the arch geometry and the way it carries load, vertical loads on arch structures
produce both vertical and horizontal reactions (Fig. 2.1), similar to horizontally applied
loads. These horizontal reactions counterbalance bending moments from vertical reac-
tions, dead loads, live loads etc., resulting in generally lower bending moments compared
to an equal span beam [5, 9]. As a result, arches are typically used only where horizontal
forces can be effectively transferred to the foundation and soil. Introducing a tie, con-
necting both ends of the arch (Fig. 2.6 (b)), also known as a bow-string (or tie-rod), can
relieve foundations of the horizontal component of the arch thrust force, however, such
practice considerably limits the structureтАЩs applicability [10].
3
==PAGE:11==
2 Theory
2.1.1
Arch classification
The structural response of an arch structure depends on the materials used, load condi-
tions, the archтАЩs shape and its static system. The overall form of the arch is defined by
its rise, span and centerline curvature, while the static system is determined by boundary
conditions, such as support and hinge arrangement. Consequently, arch structures can be
categorized based on their shape and static system.
2.1.1.1
According to shape
Historically, arch structures have utilized various centerline shapes, including circular
(semicircular, segmental or compound circular), elliptical, (second-order) parabolic, (in-
verse) catenary and in some cases even polygonal [7]. As previously established, an arch
that closely follows its thrust line under permanent load can be an economical and sustain-
able structure in terms of material consumption. However, non-uniform or live loading on
top will cause the thrust line to deviate from the archтАЩs centerline, resulting in bending
moments [10]. When a vertical uniformly distributed load is applied across the entire
span of an arch, the optimal shape is a parabola. However, if the arch is only bearing
its own weight, then an inverse catenary, also known as the chain line, is better suited to
achieve the true arch state, theoretically resulting in no bending moment and shear forces.
When considering both types of loads simultaneously, the optimal solution might be a
circular shape тАУ a close the average of the two [8]. A graphical comparison of the circular,
parabolic and catenary curves at span ratios of 1/4 and 1/10 presented in Fig. 2.2.
Figure 2.2 | Arch shape comparison for circular, parabola and catenary curves
For a fixed rise height and span length, the arch shape can be described by using the
selected shape functions described in Eq. 2.1, 2.3 and 2.4, where y is the arch height,
x is the distance along the span, h is the rise height and L represents the span length.
Additionally, Eq. 2.2 is used to calculate the radius of a circle and Eq. 2.5 is used to solve
for the parameter a, that describes the distance of the lowest point above x axis in a
regular catenary curve [6, 11].

As observed from Fig. 2.2, the distinction between the different curves becomes more
noticeable at higher rise-to-span ratios. However, at lower values where the arch flattens,
all three curves appear to converge. This indicates that for shallow arches, the exact
formulation of the arch curve becomes less significant. Consequently, a circular curve
becomes preferable in a segmental construction approach, enabling the production of
identical building elements [4, 8].
The rise-to-span ratio (h/L) is the ratio between the height of the total rise in the arch
(h) and the total length (L). The rise of an arch, and thus the rise-to-span ratio, can be
influenced by various local conditions such as the geometry of the obstacle the arch is
spanning, clearance requirements, or foundation conditions. It is an important parameter
in arch configuration for both, as established earlier, structural and aesthetic considera-
tions. Generally, neither very low nor very high rise-to-span ratios are desirable from a
combined perspective of aesthetics, economics, and construction feasibility. Regardless of
the specific arch curvature and its characteristic bending moment distribution, the rise-
to-span ratio largely determines the magnitude of the horizontal reactions at the support
locations [10]. A smaller ratio or a flat arch will result in relatively larger compressive
forces in the arch. While advantageous for arch structures made of stone or concrete,
due to the stabilizing effect of pre-compression [4], it also leads to considerably larger
horizontal support reactions, potentially requiring more massive and costly supports, de-
pending on the foundation soil conditions. Conversely, an arch with a high ratio results
in relatively smaller horizontal reactions, but generates larger bending moments due to
the increased arch length and self-weight of the structure [7].
5
==PAGE:13==
2 Theory
Figure 2.3 | Major arch bridges; comparison of rise-to-span ratios [10] (the original has been
edited to present rise/span ratio instead of the span/rise ratio for the sake of continuity)
The rise-to-span ratio typically falls within the range of 1/2 to 1/10, with the most
common cases ranging from 1/3 to 1/6 [7]. Fig. 2.3 presents the rise-to-span ratios of
several major arch bridges across the world.
2.1.1.2
According to static system
According to the static system, arch structures are classified into fixed arches, two-hinged,
and three-hinged arches as shown in Fig. 2.4. A three-hinged arch has three hinges, one
at each support of the arch and one at the crown. Allowing rotations at each hinge and
is a statically determinate structure. A two-hinged arch has one hinge at each support of
the arch and is statically indeterminate to one degree of freedom. Consequently, a fixed
arch has no hinges and is statically indeterminate to three degrees of freedom [7, 10].
Arch structures are sensitive to secondary effects, such as arch shortening, foundation
settlement, yielding of supports, and temperature variations. These effects can produce
considerable changes in the resultant forces, but the degree of sensitivity depends on the
rise-to-span ratio and the number of hinges. As the rise-to-span ratio or the number of
hinges is increased, the secondary effects tend to decrease or nearly vanish [7, 10].
Figure 2.4 | Illustration of different arch hinges: a) three-hinged arch, b) two-hinged and c)
fixed arch [7]
2.1.2
Arch bridges
An arch bridge is a bridge type that utilizes an arch as its primary load-carrying system.
Similar to other arch structures, arch bridges can be categorized based on construction
materials, static system, and shape. Additionally, they may be further classified according
6
==PAGE:14==
2 Theory
to parameters such as the relative positions of the deck and the arch rib, spandrel struc-
ture, and structural systems [7]. The typical terminology used to describe arch bridges
is depicted in Fig. 2.5.
Furthermore, arch bridges may be designed as single-span or
multi-span bridges, consisting of a series of arches with identical or varying span lengths
adjusted to the height of the bridge deck above the ground [10].
Figure 2.5 | Arch bridge nomenclature
According to the placement of the deck, arch bridges are categorized into deck arch,
through arch, and half-through arch bridges, as illustrated in Fig. 2.6.
A deck arch
bridge has its deck positioned above the crown of the arch (Fig. 2.6 (a)), which is a
typical configuration for a true arch bridge and the most common type of arch bridge.
In contrast, a through arch bridge has the deck placed at the springing line of the arch
(Fig. 2.6 (b)), with its horizontal support reactions typically carried by a tie rod system
or girder connecting the two ends of the arch, resulting in a tied arch bridge as previously
described in Section 2.1. In a through arch configuration, the loads from the deck are
transferred to the arch through tension hangers. On the other hand, if the bridge deck is
placed at an elevation above the springing line, but below the crown, it is referred to as
a half-through arch bridge (Fig. 2.6 (c)) [7, 9].
Figure 2.6 | Classification of arch bridges according to the relative positions of the deck and
arch rib: (a) deck arch bridge, (b) through arch bridge and (c) half-through arch bridge [7]
In the case of deck arch bridges, the space between the deck and the arch is called spandrel.
If this space is filled with soil or another type of filling material, the traffic load from the
deck is transferred through this filling material onto the extrados of the arch, resulting in
what is called a filled or solid spandrel arch bridge (Fig. 2.5 - left). Conversely, if there
are openings in this space, the structure is referred to as an open-spandrel arch bridge
7
==PAGE:15==
2 Theory
(Fig. 2.5 - right). In open-spandrel arch bridges, the loads from the deck are transferred
through spandrel columns or a truss-like bracing system, in which case the structure would
be classified as a braced spandrel or truss arch bridge [7, 10].
Arch structures and bridges offer several advantages. The cross-section of the primary
load-bearing structure is mainly subjected to compression, allowing for more efficient
material utilization and thus a relatively large span capacity. Additionally, arch bridges
are relatively rigid and experience minimal deformations in service state, ensuring better
driving conditions compared to other large-span capable bridge types, such as cable-stayed
bridges, and typically require less overall maintenance [9].
On the other hand, arch bridges also have their disadvantages. These include their rela-
tively large self-weight, resulting in large thrust forces, usually requiring large and costly
abutments. Most importantly, arch bridges typically pose considerable challenges in con-
struction, leading to higher costs, as arch structures do not function until the entire arch
system has been completed [7, 9].
2.1.3
Structural response
As previously established, the structural behavior of an arch structure varies depending on
its selected shape and static system. A true arch is only subjected to compressive forces in
its section, resulting in no bending moments. This is particularly the case in specific arch
shapes subjected to certain load distributions, such as a three-hinge circular arch under a
radial uniform load, a parabolic arch under a vertical uniform load, and a catenary arch
under a varying load density proportional to the axis curve elevation. However, achieving
a true arch in applications such as an arch bridge is practically impossible due to various
types of loading applied, most notably moving point loads [7].
Uniform loading is rarely the critical load condition for arch structures, as they are de-
signed to accommodate such load distribution. Critical loads on arch structures typically
involve non-symmetrically positioned loads, such as heavy point loads moving across the
span. The most critical positioning of such loads would be at the quarter point of the
span for a point load or covering one-half of the span for a uniformly distributed load [4,
8].
2.2
Pearl-Chain principle
The conventional method for constructing arches or any other curved concrete structures
involves complex temporary scaffolding systems and curved molds along with significant
labor for installation. This, being a major drawback in comparison to other structural
systems, has resulted in arch structures becoming less attractive [8].
In 2007, DTU professor Kristian D. Hertz invented light-weight load-bearing structures,
closely followed by the development of the Pearl-Chain (PC) principle. The technology is
based on the pearl-chain principle, where the precast concrete units are pulled on a steel
wire through a pre-installed cable duct and then prestressed, forming a stable arch-shaped
structure. The wires are then post-tensioned and the segments are in compression [3].
Fig. 2.7 illustrates the construction of a first-ever Pearl-Chain Arch Bridge, known as the
8
==PAGE:16==
2 Theory
Vorgod Creek Road Bridge, where a fully assembled Pearl-Chain Arch is being lifted into
its designated position [8].
Figure 2.7 | Construction of the Vorgod Creek Road Bridge [8].
2.3
Sandwich structures
Sandwich panels are multilayered structures consisting of one or more high-strength and
stiffness layers called faces (or skins, wythes), bonded to one or more flexible layers with
considerably lower density, called the core. As illustrated in Fig. 2.8, the faces are typically
made from identical materials and thicknesses. This configuration, similar to an I-beam,
results in increased strength and stiffness without a substantial increase in weight. In this
analogy, the faces act as flanges, primarily carrying load in tension/compression, while
the core, similar to a sectionтАЩs web, provides structural support, increases bending and
torsional stiffness, and carries the majority of the shear load [12].
Figure 2.8 | Illustration sandwich plate geometry [13].
2.3.1
Sandwich structure classification
Sandwich structures are categorized according to their layer composition and core as-
sembly type. According to the layer composition, three categories can be identified: an
9
==PAGE:17==
2 Theory
ordinary sandwich, consisting of two outer faces surrounding a single lightweight core (as
presented in Fig. 2.8), an open-face sandwich or semi-sandwich, consisting of only one
face and a core and a multi-layer sandwich which consists of multiple cores and facings.
Concepts for all three panel types are illustrated in Fig. 2.9.
Among these, ordinary
sandwich panels are most commonly used [12].
(a)
(b)
(c)
Figure 2.9 | (a) Ordinary [14], (b) Open-face and (c) multi-layer sandwich concepts [15].
In terms of core assembly, generally, two groups are distinguished: homogeneous and
structured core materials, as illustrated in Fig. 2.10. Homogeneous materials are further
divided into solid and foam cores, while structured core materials are categorized into
honeycomb, corrugated, and textile cores [12].
Figure 2.10 | Core assembly groups and types [14].
Sandwich structures can be constructed out of a wide range of materials for both face
and core layers, leading to a large variety of possible combinations. Their lightweight
construction has lead to increased popularity, driven by the global efforts to reduce the
carbon footprint of building construction and operation. Coupled, with advancements
in materials and the demand for high-performance, lightweight construction, sandwich
structures are expected to remain in high demand [12].
2.3.2
Composite action
To fully extract the potential of a sandwich structure, a high degree of composite action
between its components is strongly desirable. The degree of composite action in structural
10
==PAGE:18==
2 Theory
elements can be classified into fully composite, partially composite, and non-composite.
This classification is based on the shear flow capacity determined by the bond and shear
connector strength between the core and face layers (refer to Fig. 2.11) [12].
Figure 2.11 | Strain distribution at different degrees of composite action [16]
.
A section is considered fully composite when all of the longitudinal shear, as illustrated
by the linear strain profile in Fig. 2.11 (a), is transferred between the faces, resulting in all
parts of the panel working together as a unit in resisting bending moments. On the other
hand, if there is no shear transfer, the section is non-composite, as illustrated in Fig. 2.11
(c). Partial composite action falls somewhere between the two extremes, involving only a
fraction of shear transfer, thus allowing the faces to share the load but bend independently
around their neutral axes (Fig. 2.11 (b)). The schematic in Fig. 7 further illustrates the
influence of different degrees of composite action on layer interaction and the resulting
deflections [12].
Figure 2.12 | The influence of degree of composite action on load transfer and deflection [12]
An increased composite action not only increases the load-bearing capacity but also en-
sures structural predictability, enabling efficient design through analytical calculations.
Achieving full composite action in sandwich structures is rather challenging mainly due
to practical reasons related to the stiffness of shear connectors, which physically connect
the two faces and are crucial for achieving a high degree of composite action. As a result,
11
==PAGE:19==
2 Theory
partial composite action is more common, leading to structural behavior that is harder to
predict [12]. This aspect constitutes a significant research topic on its own due to its com-
plexity. Consequently, this study assumes full composite action and focuses specifically
on shape optimization.
2.3.3
Sandwich arch bridges
A sandwich arch bridge is a specialized type of closed (or filled) spandrel arch bridge that
utilizes the sandwich concept. Typically, in closed-spandrel arch bridges, the primary
load-bearing structure тАУ the arch тАУ is constructed from either in-situ cast or precast
concrete elements [4]. As the name suggests, the space between the arch and the roadway
is typically filled with loose soil such as dirt or gravel, or lightweight concrete, on top of
which the pavement, typically asphalt, is laid. This conventional arch bridge composition
is illustrated schematically in Fig. 2.13 (a). As depicted in the figure, the vertically applied
load is distributed among the different layers, however, each of them acts independently,
resulting in a non-composite action, which is sub-optimal in terms of load-bearing capacity
and material efficiency.
(a)
(b)
Figure 2.13 | (a) Conventional arch and (b) Sandwich arch bridge working principles
On the other hand, Fig. 2.13 (b) illustrates the working principle of a sandwich arch
bridge. Such a system can utilize the same arch structure as the conventional design,
but often incorporates Pearl-Chain reinforcement technology, as outlined in Section 2.2.
Additionally, it includes a post-tensioned concrete top plate and a filling layer capable of
transferring shear between the upper and lower surfaces. When sufficient bonds between
the layers and shear capacity within the filling layer are ensured, the system functions as
a composite structure, resisting loads similar to a sandwich structure (Section 2.3). In
this composite system, the arch and the top plate work together to resist applied loads
as a compression and tension force couple [6, 8]. Consequently, the load-bearing capacity
of a sandwich arch bridge relies primarily on the thickness of the arch, top plate, their
prestressing forces, and the distance between the two outer layers, which varies along the
length of a closed-spandrel arch bridge.
12
==PAGE:20==
2 Theory
2.4
Sandwich effect
The sandwich effect refers to the enhanced structural capacity achieved by integrating
different layers in a composite action, typically involving a top deck, an arch, and an
intermediate filling layer. For the sandwich arch bridge design, achieving the full benefits
of this effect requires prestressing and post-tensioning the top deck elements to effectively
manage the tensile forces. This method ensures that the entire structure behaves as a
cohesive unit, maximizing load-bearing capacity.
When loads are applied to the bridge, they induce bending moments that create both
compression and tension within the structure, particularly in the arch and top deck. For
the sandwich effect to be fully realized, these layers must interact with complete composite
action, which is assumed to be true.
Figure 2.14 | The bridge reaction to the applied load
The Fig. 2.14 above illustrates how the bridge naturally will react to the applied loads,
causing the bridge to bend downwards where the point loads are placed and where the
bending moment is positive and the other side of the bridge tries to bend upwards where
the bending moment is positive. To resist the bridge bending as in Fig. 2.14 the bridge
withstands the forces with compression where the bridge is bending upwards and tension
where the bridge is bending downwards. See Fig. 2.15
Figure 2.15 | How tension and compression are created by bending moment
The Fig. 2.15 shows how the tension and compression are made by the moment. The
section is where the point loads are applied and where the positive bending moment is
highest. The principle is for the negative bending moment.
13
==PAGE:21==
2 Theory
The maximum capacity of a sandwich bridge is found by testing the total normal force
capacity of the bridge, to the maximum normal force created by the moment. The test
would be done with both the maximum negative and positive bending moment. The
method used to find the capacity control is from a tutorial in the Super-light Structures
course called Tutorial 5 by Philip Skov Halding [17].
NPos := тИТMpos
arm
(2.6)
Nneg := тИТMneg
arm
(2.7)
The arm is the moment arm which resists the moment by taking it up as tension and
compression. The arm is the distance between the neutral axis of the top deck, to the
neutral axis of the arch. Mpos is the maximum positive bending moment and Mneg is the
maximum negative bending moment.
The capacity control to see if the bridge can withstand the applied load is shown in the
Eq. 2.8 and Eq. 2.9.
тИТNtot > Nneg
(2.8)
Ntot > Npos
(2.9)
Where Ntot is the normal force capacity in the bridge and Npos is the total normal force
created by the negative bending moment and Npos is the total normal force created by
the positive bending moment
2.5
Design limitations
This study aims to optimize the shape of a sandwich arch bridge by utilizing a curved top
plate, which requires compliance with specific parameters outlined by applicable standards
and local regulations. For road bridges, key considerations include the normal profile of
the road section and the plan and longitudinal profile of the bridge to ensure seamless
integration with the connecting roads. In this study, emphasis is placed on the longitudinal
profile as it is the parameter that can potentially result in increased material savings. To
meet regulatory requirements, the road surface and thus the top plate must meet specified
limiting values for the vertical curvature and the slope of the road surface, as further
described by the following subsections.
2.5.1
Inclination
The maximum slope permitted in the longitudinal direction within Copenhagen Munici-
pality is such that bridges and ramps should not exceed 4.5% inclination for ramps longer
than 100 meters. For shorter ramps, a steeper inclination may be used but must be in
agreement with the bridge-responsible of Copenhagen Municipality [18].
14
==PAGE:22==
2 Theory
The general requirement for the European road network is that the resulting gradient
cannot be steeper than 10%, and for ramps, the gradient should not exceed 7% uphill and
8% downhill [19].
2.5.2
Vertical curvature
2.5.2.1
Stopping sight
The stopping sight over a curve is the total length needed for a driver to observe an
approaching object and stop the vehicle. It is determined by the driverтАЩs eye height, the
object height, and the speed limit. See illustration in Fig. 4.3.
Figure 2.16 | Sketch of stopping sight over a curve [20]
The requirement for stopping sight distances for oncoming cars and pedestrians over a
curve is depicted in Table 2.1.

Table 2.1 | Limited overview [19].
If the the stopping sight distance is greater than the values in Table 2.1 the visibility is
then limited, then the bridge requires barrier lines between the driving lanes [19].
2.5.2.2
Vertical curve radius
For safety, driving comfort, and accessible roads for all vehicles, the minimum radius of a
convex curve is limited to 800 meters, and for concave 400 meters [19].
2.6
Superlight-Deck
Superlight deck combines strong heavy concrete with lightweight aggregate concrete to
create a high-performance deck.
The combinations of prestressed slabs with different
materials create some advantages such as long spans due to low weight, improved sound
insulation, low CO2 emissions, and enhanced flexibility in design [21]. Due to its low
weight, it is possible to use a crane to lift longer and larger spans,
15
==PAGE:23==
2 Theory
Figure 2.17 | Principle of a SL-deck [4]
Fig. 2.17 shows a cross-section of an SL-deck. The SL-deck is built up of light aggregate
concrete blocks covered by a layer of regular concrete with pre-tensioning strands and
post-tensioning strands. For the post-tension cable, the number and layout of the strands
are important to achieve the expected capacity of each strand. Fig. 2.18 shows the three
smallest layouts for the post-tension cable.
Figure 2.18 | Post-tension strands layout [22]
2.7
Global warming potensial
The Global Warming Potential (GWP) is a metric used to assess the amount of energy
added to the atmosphere by various greenhouse gases, expressed relative to carbon diox-
ide (C02) as the baseline standard[23]. In construction, materials like concrete and steel
have significant C02 footprints, encompassing emissions from their production, transporta-
tion, and disposal processes. The GWP values for these materials, which are essential
for evaluating their environmental impact, can be found in the Environmental Product
Declarations (EPD) Database managed by EPD Denmark [24].
2.8
Loads
Loads refer to the forces or other actions that result in stress, deformation, and displace-
ment of a structure. Accurate understanding and precise calculation of these loads are
essential to guarantee the safety, functionality, and durability of the structure. These
loads include dead loads, live loads (such as traffic loads), environmental loads (such as
wind or seismic forces), and accidental loads (such as vehicle impacts). In this thesis, the
primary considerations are dead loads and traffic loads, and both concepts will be further
described in this sub-chapter.
16
==PAGE:24==
2 Theory
2.8.1
Dead load
Dead loads are the self-weight of the structural components and any permanent fixtures
attached to the bridge. These loads are usually constant over time and will depend on
the design and structure of the bridge. The dead load of a sandwich bridge is the total
self-weight of the arch, the filling layer, and the top layer acting together. The dead load
is calculated from the density of the material, the thickness of each layer, and the width.
In this thesis, the top deck consists of prestressed concrete with a density of 2500 kg/m3,
and the arch consists of SL-decks which have a combination of lightweight concrete with
a density of 700 kg/m3 [21] and a self-compacting concrete with a density of 2400kg/m3.
The combined density of the SL-deck will depend on the thickness.
The filling layer
consists of pervious concrete and has a density of 1900 kN/m3, this is collected from
earlier studies and pervious pavement [25].
2.8.2
Traffic loads
Traffic loads represent the different situations that can appear on a bridge due to the
presence of vehicles, pedestrians, and special vehicles. The load models used in bridge
design are primarily defined by standards and codes developed by engineering bodies, with
the Eurocode being one of the key references. The Eurocode is a collection of harmonized
technical rules for the structural design of construction works in Europe, developed by
the European Committee for Standardization (CEN). Specifically, Eurocode 1 (EN 1991)
provides comprehensive guidance on how to model traffic loads for different types of
bridges [26]. EN 1991-2, which deals with traffic loads on bridges, defines several load
models to represent various traffic conditions and scenarios:
тАв Load Model 1 (LM1): Simulates normal traffic conditions, including axle loads and
uniformly distributed loads (UDL).
тАв Load Model 2 (LM2): Accounts for dynamic amplification effects, particularly im-
portant for short-span bridges.
тАв Load Model 3 (LM3): Represents special vehicles that impose unique loading con-
ditions.
тАв Load Model 4 (LM4): Used for pedestrian and cycle bridges, simulating loads im-
posed by crowds and bicycles.
In this thesis, Load Model 1 and Load Model 3 are used in the analysis.
2.8.2.1
Load model 1
Load Model 1 (LM1) includes two key components for assessing bridge loads. The first
component, known as the tandem system (TS), consists of two axle loads spaced 1.2 meters
apart. For accurate assessment, LM1 should be applied in the most critical scenario, which
typically occurs in lane 1 when considering longitudinal loading. The second component
of LM1 is the uniformly distributed load (UDL), which should be applied to the most
unfavorable side of the structure to simulate realistic load distribution
17
==PAGE:25==
2 Theory
Additionally, LM1 includes adjustment factors specific to bridge classes. These factors
modify the load values to reflect different safety requirements or design standards for
various bridge classifications.
The adjustment factors for the tandem system (TS) in
LM1 are provided in the following equations.
P1 = ╬▒Q1 ┬╖ Qk1
(2.10)
P2 = ╬▒Q2 ┬╖ Qk2
(2.11)
The adjustment factors for the bridge class 1 and 2 is ╬▒Q1 = 1 and ╬▒Q2 = 0.8, respectively,
found in Eurocode 1: Actions on structures - Part 2: Traffic loads on bridges [26] and the
point load Qk2 is 300 kN.
The adjustment factors for the UDL are shown in the equations below.
Q1 = ╬▒Q1 ┬╖ qk1
(2.12)
Q2 = ╬▒Q2 ┬╖ qk2
(2.13)
The adjustment factors are ╬▒q1 = 0.667 and ╬▒q1 = 0.333. The UDL is 9 kN/m2 in both
cases. In TS and UDL the bridge factor one is the most critical, and the one used in
further calculations.
For the calculations of the arch, the width used is smaller than the width of 2 m for the
axle load. The bridge will only be loaded by one wheel per axle. Therefore the reduction
factor kaxle = 0.5 will be used on the axle load. The Bridge will only be calculated for
lane number 1.
2.8.2.2
Load model 3
Load Model 3 accounts for special vehicles with unique loading characteristics. In this
project, we will use the 150-tonne class, as it represents the heaviest category in LM3. By
designing the bridge to accommodate the 150-tonne class, we ensure that the bridge can
safely support all potential vehicles. The specific loads and spacing associated with the
150-tonne class are illustrated in the figure below.
Figure 2.19 | Axial loads force and distances for special vehicles in class 150 tonnes [27]
2.8.3
Ultimate limit state
The Ultimate Limit State (ULS) assesses the structureтАЩs capacity to withstand extreme
conditions without failure. It ensures that the bridge can handle maximum expected loads,
including bending moments, shear forces, and compressive and tensile stresses. The ULS
check is crucial to account for uncertainties in load estimations and material properties,
ensuring that the bridge remains safe and functional under all possible scenarios. These
18
==PAGE:26==
2 Theory
checks are guided by the Eurocode standards, which provide specific criteria for different
types of structures.
The relevant load factors for ULS are specified in Annex A2 of the Eurocode, particularly
for bridge applications [28]. These load factors vary depending on whether the dead load
or the live load presents the most critical condition. In in Table 2.4 the Equation 6.10a ,
the dead load is the dominant factor, while in Equation 6.10b, the live load becomes the
primary consideration. The precise values for these factors are detailed in Table 2.2.

3 Optimization of the arch
3
Optimization of the arch
This chapter focuses on the methodology and considerations when optimizing the design
of the arch. The process aims to enhance the structural capacity and cost-effectiveness
by adjusting key design parameters. These parameters include the arch center line shape,
rise-to-span ratio, and height of the filling layer. An optimal combination of these factors
will achieve a robust and efficient bridge design. The following sub-chapter will go through
each parameter that is considered when designing the arch for the sandwich arch bridge.
For more details and calculations see Appendix A.
3.1
Arch center line shape
Different arch center line shapes are illustrated in fig Fig. 3.1. The different arch center
line shapes tested are circular, parabolic, and catenary. Each of them has unique charac-
teristics and structural advantages. The circular arch center line is favorable when using
SL-deck elements since it can use the benefit of equal design. The use of equal design
reduces production costs, simplifies assembly, and gives more effective transportation.
The disadvantage of the circular arch line shape is the increased bending moment from
loading.
The different center line shapes are tested with a uniformly distributed dead load from
the filling layer of 1 meter The filling material between the top plate and the arch will
be uniformly distributed load on the arch. To illustrate the behavior of the different arch
shapes with a filling of 1 meter over the whole arch of pervious concrete with a self-weight
of 19kN/m [25]. The difference can be seen in Fig. 3.1.
Figure 3.1 | Illustration of different center line shape
The rise-to-span ratio used in the figure is 1/10. The red curve is the circular arch, the
green one is the catenary-shaped arch and the blue curve is the parabolic-shaped arch.

1/12, and 1/14, with a circular center line of the anch. The span of the arch is L = 30
m and is a two-hinged arch. The loads calculated are the self-weight from the arch, top

4 Load models
4
Load models
When calculating a bridge structure, multiple different load types and combinations can
occur. To assume the worst-case scenarios that can happen to a bridge, the bridge will
be compared to different load models, to see how the bridge will react to each scenario.
The bridge will be tested for different load models for LM1 and LM3 in this chapter.
4.0.1
Load model comparison
To reduce the amount of load combination for further calculation, each of the different
load combinations for LM1 and LM3 is compared and the load combination with the
largest internal forces is used in further calculations. The different load combinations are
shown in Fig. 4.1.
Figure 4.1 | Load cases for LM1 and LM3 for load model comparison.
4.0.2
Critical loads
After comparing the different load combinations for both LM1 and LM3, the further
calculation will only be with the critical load model that creates the largest forces in the
arch. The different bending moment for each load case is shown in Fig. 4.2
25
==PAGE:33==
4 Load models
Figure 4.2 | Illustration of the bending moment due to different load combination
The figure above illustrates the bending moment for the different load models for both
LM1 and LM3 calculated as a 2-hinged arch. The LM1 in quarter-point, creates the
largest positive and negative bending moment in the arch. The load case with LM1 in
quarter-point will be used for further calculations since it is the critical load case. The
moment and shear from these forces will be checked against the capacity control of the
bridge.
4.1
Calculation method
For the calculations of loads and load response of the structure, the calculations have
been done as a 2-hinge arch. The optimization of the sandwich bridge has been done
by 3-hinged calculations, using a Mathcad sheet where different parameters and factors
were tested in the sheet. The reason the optimization of the sandwich bridge is done with
3-hinges is because it is advantageous due to its simplified analysis, since it is statically
determinate, making it easier to calculate the internal forces. This leads to easier hand
calculations, which allows for testing more parameters.
4.1.1
Hand calculation
The majority of the calculations are done by hand calculations in Mathcad. Mathcad
is an engineering calculation software, that makes it simple to perform and document
calculations.
4.1.2
Autodesk Robot Structural analysis
Autodesk Robot Structural analysis is a structural load analysis software, that is based on
the finite element method (FEM). FEM is a method for numerically solving differential
equations for structural analysis [30].
26
==PAGE:34==
4 Load models
Autodesk Robot has been used to find the critical load combination from the different
load models and to check the hand calculations. Since the Robot is using the FEM and
the hand calculations are using some assumptions and simplification the results will not
be the same, but the results should have some similarities to the behavior of the structure.
Simulations in Robot have been done by designing a 2-D arch with both 2-hinged and
3-hinged arch.
Figure 4.3 | Model of a 2-hinged arch in Autodesk Robot
4.1.3
Checking hand calculations with Robot (Move to results)
To check if the critical loads are calculated correctly the results from hand calculations are
checked with results from Robot Structural analysis. Test the results from Load Model 1
with the point load in the quarter point and load model 3 where the rear axles are in the
quarter point.
Figure 4.4 | LM1 traffic load in quarter-point from Robot 2-hinged
From Fig. 4.4 the total maximal bending moment for a 2-hinged arch calculated in Robot
is 675 kN/m from the axle load and 140 kN/m from the UDL load. The total bending
moment created by LM1 in quarter-point with a 2-hinge arch is Mmax = 815kN/m.
Figure 4.5 | LM1 traffic load in quarter-point from Robot 3-hinged
From Fig. 4.5 the total maximal bending moment for a 3-hinged arch calculated in Robot
is 774 kN/m from the axle load and 140 kN/m from the UDL load. The total bending
moment created by LM1 in quarter-point with a 3-hinge arch is Mmax = 914kN/m.

5 Loads and load response
5
Loads and load response
This chapter shows the bridge static system and how the internal forces react to the
applied load for a 2-hinged arch bridge. The reaction of the internal force is shown for the
dead load and the live load. The internal forces are tested with different load combinations
in ULS to find the most critical load combination. The Critical load combination will be
used to check capacity of the bridge.
5.1
Static system of the Bridge
The static system of the bridge is that the arch is supported by a horizontal and vertical
force at each end of the arch.
Figure 5.1 | Illustration Reaction forces of the sandwich bridge
Since the structure is indeterminate it requires some more advanced calculations to find
the horizontal reaction forces in the supports. Different methods and formulas exist to
find the horizontal reaction force. The formulas used to find the horizontal force are from
[31] and are shown below.

Eq. 5.1 calculates the horizontal reaction force from a single point load for a 2-hinge arch.
The x is the distance away from reaction A. P is the point load in kN, L is the total length
of the span and f is the total rise of the arch.
RH.A = wL2
8f
(5.2)
Method to find the horizontal force from UDL over the whole span for a 2-hinge arch,
where w is the UDL. To find the horizontal force from the different load combinations,
itтАЩs been used a combination of Eq. 5.1 and Eq. 5.2.
5.2
Structural reaction to dead load
The dead load from the whole structure will only be carried by the PC arch. Since the
filling layer, the arch and top deck have a constant height over the whole span. The dead
load acts as a UDL over the entire span.
30
==PAGE:38==
5 Loads and load response
5.2.1
Bending moment due to dead load
The bending moment due to dead load is tested with the different load combinations.
The highest positive and negative bending moment from the dead load is created by LC2,
which is where all the dead load is unfavorable. The bending moment due to dead load
is shown in the figure below.
Figure 5.2 | Illustration of the bending moment due to dead load
The Fig. 5.2 shows the bending moment due to dead load over the distance of the bridge,
for a 2-hinge arch. The MEdA.1(x) is the bending moment where all the dead load is
unfavorable, for MEdA.2(x) the filling layer is favorable, and the arch and top plate is
unfavorable. The MEdA.4(x) all the dead load is favorable.
5.2.2
Shear force due to dead load
In an arch, shear force refers to the internal force that acts perpendicular to the archтАЩs
axis. It plays a crucial role in maintaining the structural integrity of the arch, particularly
under asymmetric loads, by ensuring stability throughout the span and counteracting
lateral movements.
31
==PAGE:39==
5 Loads and load response
Figure 5.3 | Illustration of the shear force due to dead load
The Fig. 5.3 shows the shear force created by the dead load for load combinations 1, 2,
and 3. Load combination 1 with all dead loads unfavorable is the critical load case.
5.3
Structural reaction to live load and dead load
The load response from live loads results in bending moments, which are resisted by a
couple of forces within the arch and the top plate. The shear forces, on the other hand,
are assumed to be resisted by the pervious concrete in the filling layer.
Figure 5.4 | Live load response reaction in a sandwich bridge

Bending moment due to live load
In the figure below the critical load model is LM1 in quarter point with dead load of the
total structure. The figure shows the bending moment for both load case 3 where the
dead load is unfavorable and load case 4 where the dead load is favorable.

6 Sandwich arch bridge
6
Sandwich arch bridge
In this chapter, the sandwich arch will be tested with different parameters to find the
effect of each parameter. The findings from Section 3 will be used as base parameters.
The bridge will be tested with different parameters to see how the capacity of a sandwich
bridge will react when changing a parameter.
The method used to test out the different parameters, is using Appendix C, which consists
of a Mathcad Sheet that calculates a sandwich arch bridge as a 3-hinged arch.
The
different parameters that have been tested are the height of the filling layer, arch element
and the top deck, number of strands in post-tensioning, rise-to-span ratio, and density of
the filling material.
6.1
Ground parameters of the bridge
The sandwich bridge is tested by adjusting different parameters to see how it will affect
the sandwich bridgeтАЩs total capacity against the critical load.
The bridge will be checked with the same parameters for all the tests, except the height
of the filling layer. The height of the filling layer will be mentioned before each test.
The ground parameters used to test the effect of different parameters are shown in the
table below.

6.1.1
Capacity control
The bridge will be tested against the critical load LM1 in quarter point. The bridge
will be checked for maximum positive and negative bending moments, and check if the
cross-section can handle the compression and tension made by the bending moment in
each quarter point.

of the filling material, the total amount of Post-tension strands in the arch, the rise-to-span
ratio, the height of the arch, and the top plate element.

7 Optimal sandwich arch structure
7
Optimal sandwich arch structure
Building on the findings from the previous chapter, this chapter selects the optimal values
for the various parameters, prioritizing material efficiency and emission reduction. The
revised design is compared to the original in terms of Global Warming Potential (GWP).
Finally, practical considerations for implementing the new design are discussed.
7.1
The difference between the old and new design of the sand-
wich bridge
7.1.1
The old design
The old design, where the top layer is flat and there is a filling layer between the arch
and the top layer, requires a large amount of filling at the end sides. The weak point of
the bridge is above the crown where the filling layer has the lowest height.
Figure 7.1 | The sandwich bridge with flat top deck
7.1.2
The new design
The new design incorporates a top plate that matches the radius and curvature of the
arch, reducing the amount of filling material required on the sides of the bridge. However,
this design shifts the weak point to the quarter point of the bridge, where the critical
moment from live loads occurs. Since the filling height remains the same across the whole
bridge, it results in areas that are less utilized where the bending moment is lower.
Figure 7.2 | The sandwich bridge with curved top deck
7.2
Design of the sandwich arch bridge
7.2.1
Span-to-rise ratio
The span-to-rise ratio used is 1/14, since changing the span-to-rise ratio has an exponential
difference in the capacity of the bridge, the only downside is that the horizontal force also
rises exponentially when reducing the rise-to-span ratio.
In 3.3 it was chosen a span-to-rise ratio of 1/10 since any lower ratios would have made
a large horizontal force on the supports of the bridge. Since the filling height could be
41
==PAGE:49==
7 Optimal sandwich arch structure
reduced much more than anticipated the total dead load of the structure was reduced
enough to change the rise-to-span ratio to 1/14 and still have around the horizontal force
in the reactions.
The span-to-rise span could be lowered even more for the benefit of the cross-section of
the bridge but would move the problems down to the foundation with large horizontal
forces. Since the foundation will not be checked, the rise-to-span ratio of 1/14 is a sensible
choice based on the information we have.
7.2.2
Post-tensioning strands
When selecting the number of strands for post-tensioning, itтАЩs essential to account for both
the structural requirements and practical considerations. The arrangement of strands
must not only meet the desired load-bearing capacity but also be feasible in terms of
installation and structural design. As highlighted earlier in Fig. 2.18, strands can only
be arranged in certain groups (3,4,7,9,...). As shown in Fig. 6.3, the capacity difference
between using 4 strands and 7 strands is quite significant, amounting to nearly 500 kN.
This large gap means that there is a substantial increase in the load-bearing capacity
when moving from 4 to 7 strands. However, even with a 1-meter height filling layer,
configurations using 3 or 4 strands fall short of meeting the required capacity for the
structure. This insufficiency necessitates considering the next available strand configura-
tion. If more than 7 strands are required to achieve the necessary capacity, the subsequent
layout option jumps to 9 strands. This increase is also quite large and should be carefully
evaluated to ensure it aligns with the structural needs and budgetary constraints of the
project.
The CO2 emissions associated with the choice of the number of strands are not a significant
factor in the decision-making process, as the emissions per strand are relatively minimal.
For instance, a single strand with a length of 27 meters contributes only 30 kg of CO2,
which is negligible in comparison to the overall environmental impact of the entire bridge
structure. Consequently, the primary consideration in selecting the number of strands
is structural capacity rather than environmental impact. For the design of the curved
sandwich arch bridge, it has been decided to use 7 strands to ensure sufficient load-bearing
capacity and structural integrity.
7.2.3
Arch element height
Selecting the height for the arch element is a decision that influences the bridgeтАЩs struc-
tural integrity, and total capacity of the bridge. The height of the arch element directly
impacts the ability to withstand forces and moments. A higher arch element provides
more strength but uses more material, which creates more CO2.
The decision to use a 320 mm height is based on the requirement for the arch to bear the
majority of the forces, both during and after the bridgeтАЩs construction. This dimension
ensures the archтАЩs strength and stability under varying loads. Therefore it may be wise
to use a slightly larger arch and limit more on the other parts of the structure. The 320
mm arch element is a standardized production size of precast elements, which is more
cost-efficient than a special-made size.
42
==PAGE:50==
7 Optimal sandwich arch structure
7.2.4
Top plate element height
The top plateтАЩs main purpose is to take up tension and compression made by the moment.
The top plate primarily handles tension and compression forces. While it can be thinner
than the arch element, it must still provide sufficient strength.
The height of the arch also affects the total environmental impact of the bridge. A higher
arch requires more material. Therefore it is important to balance the material use and
structural efficiency.
The chosen top plate element size is 270 mm in height, which is also a standardized
production size for precast elements.
7.2.5
Filling height
The filling height in a sandwich arch bridge is a critical parameter influencing both struc-
tural performance and material efficiency.
The filling layer helps distribute the loads
between the top deck and the arch and contributes to the overall stiffness of the bridge.
From Fig. 6.2 the effect of filling height greatly impacts both the bridgeтАЩs capacity and the
total compression and tension from the bending moment. From Eq. 2.6 and Eq. 2.7 the
total normal force acting on the bridge is dependent on the lever arm. When increasing
the filling layer the lever arm increases.
Regarding the CO2 emission, the height of the filling plays a significant role. A higher
filling height increases the structural capacity, this makes it possible to reduce other
elements such as the arch and the top plate which have a higher density of concrete. The
choice of filling material is also an important factor, see Section 7.2.6
The filling height is found to be the most efficient parameter to increase the bearing
capacity of the sandwich arch bridge. The height is chosen to be 300 mm since we get
the effect of the lever arm without using too much material.
7.2.6
Filling density
The use of a filling layer with higher density is seen as favorable for the capacity of the
bridge. With a high filling height, the capacity increases the total capacity, but with a
lower filling height, the difference is marginal. The downside of using a higher-density
material is that the CO2 emission increases per cubic meter used. The CO2 emission for
the previous concrete is 247 kg/m3 while for high-density concrete it is 541 kg/m3, more
than double the CO2 emissions per cubic meter.
The chosen design will use a filling layer with pervious concrete that has a density of 19
kN/m3, because the increase of CO2 emission exceeds the advantages of increasing the
capcaity.
7.3
Hinges
For the calculations of testing different parameters, it was used a 3-hinged arch. This is
because a 3-hinged arch can serve as a simplified analytic approach for a 2-hinged arch.
43
==PAGE:51==
7 Optimal sandwich arch structure
Making the complexity more simple, allows for easier understanding of the load distribu-
tion and the behavior of the structure. Thus it is possible to obtain more calculations
and data for the effect of the sandwich behavior.
Using a 3-hinged arch is considered conservative when the Sandwich bridge is a 2-hinge
arch. In reality, the sandwich arch bridge will act as something between a 2-hinged arch
and a fixed arch. Which makes the 3-hinges calculation even more conservative.
7.4
Different spans
The bridge has been designed for 27 m, 35 m, and 50 m. The following parameters have
been changed to be within the requirements for each length.

7.5
Comparing old and new design for GWP
To evaluate the old and the new design, the total Global Warming Potential (GWP)
associated with constructing the bridge will be compared. This comparison will be con-
ducted across three different span lengths: 27 m, 35 m, and 50 m, with the bridge width
consistently set at 11.4 m for all spans. Detailed GWP calculations for each bridge length
can be found in Appendix D. The GWP values for the old design are sourced from [6].
The GWP calculations will not include transportation and disposal emissions, as these
are variable factors that depend on the specific location of the bridge construction and
the proximity to the nearest concrete manufacturer. Instead, the GWP assessment will
focus solely on the quantity and types of materials used in the bridgeтАЩs construction.
7.5.1
Material GWP
The concrete used in the top plate is a C35 which has a GWP of 362 CO2/m3 [32], The
SL-deck uses C55 concrete.
EPD Danmark doesnтАЩt have any GWP numbers for C55
concrete. To find the GWP for other concrete strengths, it is assumed that the GWP is
a linear progression between the values of C35 and C45. The values for C45 concrete is
426 CO2/m3 [33]
To find the GWP value for any type of concrete, first need to find the average of GWP
numbers of C35 and C45. Then divide the average on

23.07
35
250,011
129,622
120,389
48.16
50
391,044
201,636
189,407
48.43
Figure 7.3 | Comparison of GWP of old and new design
From Table 7.2 and Fig. 7.3, it is evident that the total difference in CO2 emissions
between the two designs is quite substantial. For a bridge span of 27 m, the GWP is 23%
lower for the new design. The difference increases significantly with a longer span. At 35
m, CO2 emissions are reduced by 48%. For a 50 m span, the CO2 reduction remains at
48% based on the GWP values provided by Flaa and Tjetland. However, it is important
to note that their report specifies a filling height of 600 mm above the crown for the 50
m span, but their GWP calculations use a filling height of only 150 mm above the crown.
This discrepancy suggests that the actual CO2 emissions for the sandwich bridge with a
flat top deck would be significantly higher if the correct filling height were accounted for.
The additional filling layer would contribute an extra 63.4 tonnes of CO2, increasing the
overall CO2 reduction to 55% with the new curved top deck design for the 50 m span.
7.6
Practical considerations
This project has intentionally disregarded the regulations outlined in Section 2.5 to explore
the most effective design concerning emissions.
To align with these regulations, one
proposed solution is to add filling material or another type of infill at the ends of the
bridge.
This modification would adjust the radius of the top plate, thereby reducing
the incline. Although this adjustment would slightly increase the total CO2 emissions,
the emissions would still be lower than those associated with a traditional sandwich arch
bridge featuring a flat top deck. Fig. 7.4 illustrates the added filling material on the sides
of the bridge.
46
==PAGE:54==
7 Optimal sandwich arch structure
Figure 7.4 | Realistic design with filling on top of the bridge
Another possibility is to have a larger radius on the top plate to make the inclination
smaller. This will increase the height of filling in the back spans, but still be more CO2
efficient than with a flat top deck.
Figure 7.5 | Realistic design with extra filling on the sides
Both Fig. 7.4 and Fig. 7.4 are a solution to solve the maximum steepness and the minimum
vertical curvature. This will also increase the total CO2 emission, but still be more CO2
efficient than with a flat top deck.
7.6.1
Degree of composite action
As mentioned in Section 2.3 the degree of composite action is assumed to be full. Earlier
master thesis tested splitting between each layer in a composite sandwich bridge and had
a problem with splitting between the layers [38]. For a realistic approach, it may be
necessary to have something to keep the layers together. A potential solution to prevent
the splitting of each layer can be adding shear rods on each side of the bridge. This is to
compress each layer together, and then potentially prevent splitting of the layers.
Figure 7.6 | Relistic design with steel rods
The figure above shows a proposal of a design of how the steel rods are placed on the side
of the bridge. spread evenly to compress each layer together.
7.6.2
User usage
The new design of the bridge, canтАЩt be directly compared to the old design with a flat
top deck. A curved top deck will affect the usage of the bridge, as the speed limit will
decrease, and where the bridge can be used.
47
==PAGE:55==
8 Conclusion
8
Conclusion
The objective of this thesis was to optimize the PC sandwich bridge top deckтАЩs shape to
reduce the total height of the filling layer at the ends of the sandwich structure. Where the
hypothesis was that the width of a curved top deck would reduce the total CO2 emission
for the bridge.
Through analytic investigations, it is found that the optimal rise-to-span ratio for a 2-
hinged sandwich arch bridge is between 1/10 and 1/14. Increasing the height of the filling
is preferable for the bending moment, but increase the horizontal force at the support.
The optimal arch center line is circular when using prefabricated PC elements since all the
elements can be produced similarly. Using a low-density filling material is more effective
than high-density because of the small advantages of using high density with a low filling
height, but it increases the self-weight and CO2 emission.
When testing the effect of different parameters for a curved sandwich arch bridge, in-
creasing the height of the lever arm is the most effective and beneficial change since it
increases both the total capacity of the bridge and decreases stresses acting on the bridge.
Where the increase of the filling layer is the most effective parameter. The effect of the
rise-to-span ratio is also a parameter that is effective for the capacity of the bridge, where
with a lower rise-to-span ratio the capacity increases. The downside is that the horizontal
load increases equally as the capacity increases.
The comparison of global warming potential between a sandwich bridge with a flat and
curved top deck. There is a significant difference between each design where a curved top
deck was in favor of both short a long spans. For a span of 27 m the sandwich bridge
with a curved top deck reduces the GWP by 23 %. When increasing the span, the curved
top deck is even more effective. When increasing the span to 35 m the total reduction is
46 % and increasing the span to 50 m the total reduction is 55 %.
The two bridges canтАЩt be directly compared since the user usage will be different. The
sandwich bridge with a flat top deck, can be used anywhere and doesnтАЩt have any limit
for the speed while driving over it. but with a curved top deck, the speed would probably
be reduced and then only used for roads with lower speed limits.
To make the bridge more comparable and a more practical consideration the radius of
the top plate must be increased to minimize the vertical radius and the incline. This will
increase the total CO2 emission, but still be more CO2 efficient than with a flat top deck.
48
==PAGE:56==
9 Further work
9
Further work
There where several subjects that were found during this thesis, that could be interesting
to investigate further.
тАв Look into how the fundaments can take up the horizontal forces from the Bridge.
тАв Research the splitting force between each layer, is it possible to add tension rods?
тАв Is there a solution to be within the requirements of the vertical curvature? Either
with a different radius or use a different curvature on the top deck.
